        <title>Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production | BMC Genomics | Full Text</title>

        

    <meta name="citation_abstract" content="Microalgae are gaining importance as sustainable production hosts in the fields of biotechnology and bioenergy. A robust biomass accumulating strain of the genus Monoraphidium (SAG 48.87) was investigated in this work as a potential feedstock for biofuel production. The genome was sequenced, annotated, and key enzymes for triacylglycerol formation were elucidated. Monoraphidium neglectum was identified as an oleaginous species with favourable growth characteristics as well as a high potential for crude oil production, based on neutral lipid contents of approximately 21% (dry weight) under nitrogen starvation, composed of predominantly C18:1 and C16:0 fatty acids. Further characterization revealed growth in a relatively wide pH range and salt concentrations of up to 1.0% NaCl, in which the cells exhibited larger structures. This first full genome sequencing of a member of the Selenastraceae revealed a diploid, approximately 68 Mbp genome with a G + C content of 64.7%. The circular chloroplast genome was assembled to a 135,362&#160;bp single contig, containing 67 protein-coding genes. The assembly of the mitochondrial genome resulted in two contigs with an approximate total size of 94&#160;kb, the largest known mitochondrial genome within algae. 16,761 protein-coding genes were assigned to the nuclear genome. Comparison of gene sets with respect to functional categories revealed a higher gene number assigned to the category &#8220;carbohydrate metabolic process&#8221; and in &#8220;fatty acid biosynthetic process&#8221; in M. neglectum when compared to Chlamydomonas reinhardtii and Nannochloropsis gaditana, indicating a higher metabolic diversity for applications in carbohydrate conversions of biotechnological relevance. The genome of M. neglectum, as well as the metabolic reconstruction of crucial lipid pathways, provides new insights into the diversity of the lipid metabolism in microalgae. The results of this work provide a platform to encourage the development of this strain for biotechnological applications and production concepts."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://bmcgenomics.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="BMC Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga Monoraphidium neglectum from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Microalgae are gaining importance as sustainable production hosts in the fields of biotechnology and bioenergy. A robust biomass accumulating strain of the genus Monoraphidium (SAG 48.87) was investigated in this work as a potential feedstock for biofuel production. The genome was sequenced, annotated, and key enzymes for triacylglycerol formation were elucidated. Monoraphidium neglectum was identified as an oleaginous species with favourable growth characteristics as well as a high potential for crude oil production, based on neutral lipid contents of approximately 21% (dry weight) under nitrogen starvation, composed of predominantly C18:1 and C16:0 fatty acids. Further characterization revealed growth in a relatively wide pH range and salt concentrations of up to 1.0% NaCl, in which the cells exhibited larger structures. This first full genome sequencing of a member of the Selenastraceae revealed a diploid, approximately 68 Mbp genome with a G + C content of 64.7%. The circular chloroplast genome was assembled to a 135,362&amp;nbsp;bp single contig, containing 67 protein-coding genes. The assembly of the mitochondrial genome resulted in two contigs with an approximate total size of 94&amp;nbsp;kb, the largest known mitochondrial genome within algae. 16,761 protein-coding genes were assigned to the nuclear genome. Comparison of gene sets with respect to functional categories revealed a higher gene number assigned to the category carbohydrate metabolic process and in fatty acid biosynthetic process in M. neglectum when compared to Chlamydomonas reinhardtii and Nannochloropsis gaditana, indicating a higher metabolic diversity for applications in carbohydrate conversions of biotechnological relevance. The genome of M. neglectum, as well as the metabolic reconstruction of crucial lipid pathways, provides new insights into the diversity of the lipid metabolism in microalgae. The results of this work provide a platform to encourage the development of this strain for biotechnological applications and production concepts."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/12864.jpg"/>

                        <h1 class="c-article-title" data-test="article-title" data-article-title="" itemprop="name headline">Reconstruction of the lipid metabolism for the microalga <i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> from its genome sequence reveals characteristics suitable for biofuel production</h1>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Background</h3><p>Microalgae are gaining importance as sustainable production hosts in the fields of biotechnology and bioenergy. A robust biomass accumulating strain of the genus <i>Monoraphidium</i> (SAG 48.87) was investigated in this work as a potential feedstock for biofuel production. The genome was sequenced, annotated, and key enzymes for triacylglycerol formation were elucidated.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Results</h3><p><i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> was identified as an oleaginous species with favourable growth characteristics as well as a high potential for crude oil production, based on neutral lipid contents of approximately 21% (dry weight) under nitrogen starvation, composed of predominantly C18:1 and C16:0 fatty acids. Further characterization revealed growth in a relatively wide pH range and salt concentrations of up to 1.0% NaCl, in which the cells exhibited larger structures. This first full genome sequencing of a member of the Selenastraceae revealed a diploid, approximately 68 Mbp genome with a G + C content of 64.7%. The circular chloroplast genome was assembled to a 135,362bp single contig, containing 67 protein-coding genes. The assembly of the mitochondrial genome resulted in two contigs with an approximate total size of 94kb, the largest known mitochondrial genome within algae. 16,761 protein-coding genes were assigned to the nuclear genome. Comparison of gene sets with respect to functional categories revealed a higher gene number assigned to the category carbohydrate metabolic process and in fatty acid biosynthetic process in <i>M. neglectum</i> when compared to <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> and <i>Nannochloropsis gaditana</i>, indicating a higher metabolic diversity for applications in carbohydrate conversions of biotechnological relevance.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" data-test="abstract-sub-heading">Conclusions</h3><p>The genome of <i>M. neglectum,</i> as well as the metabolic reconstruction of crucial lipid pathways, provides new insights into the diversity of the lipid metabolism in microalgae. The results of this work provide a platform to encourage the development of this strain for biotechnological applications and production concepts.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Phototrophic microalgae are increasingly investigated for their use in biotechnological applications as these unicellular organisms offer the opportunity of introducing sustainable production pathways by converting sunlight energy and CO<sub>2</sub> into valuable products [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schenk PMP, Thomas-Hall SR, Evan S, Marx U, Mussgnug JH, Posten C, Kruse O, Hankamer B: Second generation biofuels: high-efficiency microalgae for biodiesel production. BioEnergy Res. 2008, 1 (1): 20-43. 10.1007/s12155-008-9008-8." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64054e643">1</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mata TM, Martins ANA, Caetano NS: Microalgae for biodiesel production and other applications: A review. Renew Sust Energ Rev. 2010, 14 (1): 217-232. 10.1016/j.rser.2009.07.020." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d64054e646">5</a>]. In order to establish highly efficient strains, however, the systematic genome analysis and the development of molecular tools for genetic engineering approaches are necessary. Consequently the genomes of a number of strains of interest have recently been sequenced and essential genetic tools have been successfully introduced [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Vieler A, Wu G, Tsai C-H, Bullard B, Cornish AJ, Harvey C, Reca I-B, Thornburg C, Achawanantakun R, Buehl CJ, et al: Genome, functional gene annotation, and nuclear transformation of the Heterokont oleaginous alga Nannochloropsis oceanica CCMP1779. PLoS Genet. 2012, 8 (11): e1003064-10.1371/journal.pgen.1003064." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d64054e649">6</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e652">8</a>].</p><p>A particular focus has been placed on species with high oil content. Many microalgae are reported to produce considerable amounts of oils [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e658">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chisti Y: Biodiesel from microalgae. Biotechnol Adv. 2007, 25 (3): 294-306. 10.1016/j.biotechadv.2007.02.001." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64054e661">3</a>], where the compound of interest for biofuels is mainly triacylglycerol (TAG) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e664">2</a>]. TAG consists of three fatty acids esterified to a glycerol backbone [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chisti Y: Biodiesel from microalgae. Biotechnol Adv. 2007, 25 (3): 294-306. 10.1016/j.biotechadv.2007.02.001." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d64054e667">3</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mata TM, Martins ANA, Caetano NS: Microalgae for biodiesel production and other applications: A review. Renew Sust Energ Rev. 2010, 14 (1): 217-232. 10.1016/j.rser.2009.07.020." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d64054e670">5</a>]. The accumulation of TAG in microalgae usually occurs under environmental stress conditions such as high-light or nitrogen starvation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e674">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gong Y, Jiang M: Biodiesel production with microalgae as feedstock: from strains to biodiesel. Biotechnol Lett. 2011, 33 (7): 1269-1284. 10.1007/s10529-011-0574-z." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d64054e677">9</a>] but, however, can be also increased artificially, for example, by inhibition of starch synthesis [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Li Y, Han D, Hu G, Sommerfeld M, Hu Q: Inhibition of starch synthesis results in overproduction of lipids in Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2010, 107 (2): 258-268. 10.1002/bit.22807." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d64054e680">10</a>]. Though the lipid metabolism in algae and plants is considered to be comparatively similar, a number of differences can be found. For example, in contrast to unicellular microalgae, TAG synthesis in plants predominantly takes place in specialized tissues or organs [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e683">2</a>]. Furthermore, current results indicate not only the presence of clear differences between plants and microalgae, but also between different microalgal species [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Liu B, Benning C: Lipid metabolism in microalgae distinguishes itself. Curr Opin Biotechnol. 2013, 24 (2): 300-309. 10.1016/j.copbio.2012.08.008." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d64054e686">11</a>]. Microalgae exhibit high levels of diversity between species due to their different evolutionary history, thus it is expected that the lipid metabolism amongst the various strains also exhibits distinct differences. This is exemplified by the comparison of the green algal model organism <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> with <i>Nannochloropsis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Liu B, Benning C: Lipid metabolism in microalgae distinguishes itself. Curr Opin Biotechnol. 2013, 24 (2): 300-309. 10.1016/j.copbio.2012.08.008." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d64054e696">11</a>]. Variable lipid metabolism within the microalgae is also suggested by the high diversity of lipids of different classes and unusual fatty acids found in individual algae strains, even among the same division [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Harwood JL, Guschina IA: The versatility of algae and their lipid metabolism. Biochimie. 2009, 91 (6): 679-684. 10.1016/j.biochi.2008.11.004." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d64054e699">12</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lang I, Hodac L, Friedl T, Feussner I: Fatty acid profiles and their distribution patterns in microalgae: a comprehensive analysis of more than 2000 strains from the SAG culture collection. BMC Plant Biol. 2011, 11 (1): 124-10.1186/1471-2229-11-124." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d64054e702">13</a>].</p><p>Based on this knowledge, numerous systematic screens aiming to identify microalgal species with high lipid content have been performed during recent years [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lim DKY, Garg S, Timmins M, Zhang ESB, Thomas-Hall SR, Schuhmann H, Li Y, Schenk PM: Isolation and evaluation of oil-producing microalgae from subtropical coastal and brackish waters. PLoS ONE. 2012, 7 (7): e40751-10.1371/journal.pone.0040751." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d64054e708">14</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e711">19</a>]. With the identification of strains of interest by these approaches, the necessity for systematic analyses of genomes by next-generation sequencing, annotation and reconstruction of lipid metabolic pathways becomes evident. These strategies allow deeper insights into the lipid metabolism and evolutionary strategies of these photosynthetic microorganisms.</p><p>The genome of the green algal model organism <i>C. reinhardtii</i> has been sequenced [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e720">8</a>], as well as the genome of the TAG-accumulating Eustigmatophytes <i>Nannochloropsis gaditana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Jinkerson RE, Radakovits R, Posewitz MC: Genomic insights from the oleaginous model alga Nannochloropsis gaditana. Bioengineered. 2013, 4 (1): 37-43. 10.4161/bioe.21880." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d64054e726">20</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e729">21</a>], <i>Nannochloropsis oceanica</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Vieler A, Wu G, Tsai C-H, Bullard B, Cornish AJ, Harvey C, Reca I-B, Thornburg C, Achawanantakun R, Buehl CJ, et al: Genome, functional gene annotation, and nuclear transformation of the Heterokont oleaginous alga Nannochloropsis oceanica CCMP1779. PLoS Genet. 2012, 8 (11): e1003064-10.1371/journal.pgen.1003064." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d64054e736">6</a>], and several other microalgae [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Sasso S, Pohnert G, Lohr M, Mittag M, Hertweck C: Microalgae in the postgenomic era: a blooming reservoir for new natural products. FEMS Microbiol Rev. 2012, 36 (4): 761-785. 10.1111/j.1574-6976.2011.00304.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d64054e739">22</a>].</p><p>In our laboratory, we recently succeeded in identifying a strain of the genus <i>Monoraphidium</i> with high fatty acid abundances combined with robust biomass accumulation through a screening strategy which combined up-scaling tests, determination of total lipids, and the evaluation of fatty acid abundances [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e749">19</a>]. The identification of <i>Monoraphidium contortum</i> from this screening is in coincidence with rising interest in this genus over recent years [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Chaichalerm S, Pokethitiyook P, Yuan W, Meetam M, Sritong K, Pugkaew W, Kungvansaichol K, Kruatrachue M, Damrongphol P: Culture of microalgal strains isolated from natural habitats in Thailand in various enriched media. Appl Energy. 2012, 89 (1): 296-302. 10.1016/j.apenergy.2011.07.028." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d64054e755">23</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yu X, Zhao P, He C, Li J, Tang X, Zhou J, Huang Z: Isolation of a novel strain of Monoraphidium sp. and characterization of its potential application as biodiesel feedstock. Bioresource Technol. 2012, 121: 256-262." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d64054e758">24</a>]. Strains of the genus <i>Monoraphidium</i> belong to the family of the <i>Selenastraceae</i> within the class <i>Chlorophyceae</i>. So far, little information is available on genomes of members of the family <i>Selenastraceae</i> and related species such as <i>Scenedesmus obliquus</i>. In the light of the fact that the biodiversity of microalgae is tremendous, much of the potential for strain identification and characterisation to contribute to liquid biofuel purposes remains to be explored [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e777">2</a>].</p><p>Based on 18S rRNA analysis, it was shown that species of this genus tightly cluster with species of <i>Ankistrodesmus</i>, <i>Kirchneriella</i>, and other genera, thus not forming a monophyletic group [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Krienitz L, Ustinova I, Friedl T, Huss VAR: Traditional generic concepts versus 18S rRNA gene phylogeny in the green algal family Selenastraceae (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta). J Phycol. 2001, 37 (5): 852-865. 10.1046/j.1529-8817.2001.01004.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d64054e789">25</a>]. When <i>M. contortum,</i> isolated from a natural habitat in Thailand, was grown in different autotrophic media, it showed comparatively high lipid productivities among the strains under the investigated conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Chaichalerm S, Pokethitiyook P, Yuan W, Meetam M, Sritong K, Pugkaew W, Kungvansaichol K, Kruatrachue M, Damrongphol P: Culture of microalgal strains isolated from natural habitats in Thailand in various enriched media. Appl Energy. 2012, 89 (1): 296-302. 10.1016/j.apenergy.2011.07.028." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d64054e795">23</a>].</p><p><i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> was also identified as a robust strain belonging to the family Selenastraceae, by our previous screening efforts [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e803">19</a>]. <i>M. neglectum</i> was shown to cluster differently to <i>M. contortum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yu X, Zhao P, He C, Li J, Tang X, Zhou J, Huang Z: Isolation of a novel strain of Monoraphidium sp. and characterization of its potential application as biodiesel feedstock. Bioresource Technol. 2012, 121: 256-262." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d64054e812">24</a>], on the same branch as <i>Podohedriella falcata</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Krienitz L, Ustinova I, Friedl T, Huss VAR: Traditional generic concepts versus 18S rRNA gene phylogeny in the green algal family Selenastraceae (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta). J Phycol. 2001, 37 (5): 852-865. 10.1046/j.1529-8817.2001.01004.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d64054e819">25</a>], also known as <i>Ankistrodesmus falcatus</i>. Another strain of this species was reported as an oleaginous organism before [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Griffiths MJ, Harrison STL: Lipid productivity as a key characteristic for choosing algal species for biodiesel production. J Appl Phycol. 2009, 21 (5): 493-507. 10.1007/s10811-008-9392-7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d64054e825">26</a>], highlighting the need for more detailed comparisons and investigations among the members of the <i>Selenastraceae</i> by metabolomic studies and whole genome sequencing. When directly compared to <i>M. contortum</i>, <i>M. neglectum</i> showed some phenotypical characteristics such as higher robustness in up-scale cultivations, making this strain even more interesting for biotechnological applications.</p><p>In this work, we intensively characterised the oleaginous phenotype of <i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> and established and annotated a draft genome of this organism as a precondition for metabolic network reconstruction. Investigation of the reconstructed metabolic pathways with respect to key enzymes for triacylglycerol formation was carried out, setting the basis for further investigations and offering the possibility to develop strategies for genetic improvements. In addition, <i>M. neglectum</i> was further biochemically characterised as a robust production strain suitable for biotechnological approaches.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>The species <i>M. neglectum</i> was identified in a previous screen for oleaginous microalgal strains that exhibited robust growth characteristics at varying cultivation conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e857">19</a>]. To evaluate the biotechnological potential of this strain, a detailed investigation was performed in this work. This investigation included detailed physiological analysis, evaluating lipid yields and robustness. Genome sequencing and annotation were performed and combined with the reconstruction of phylogeny as well as an analysis of lipid metabolism pathways, features crucial for understanding and establishing <i>M. neglectum</i> as lipid-producing feedstock.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3"><i>M. neglectum</i>exhibits a rapid photoautotrophic growth phenotype and accumulates neutral lipids to a high extent under nitrogen starvation</h3><p>The combination of efficient phototrophic biomass accumulation and high neutral lipid content is considered as one of the most essential traits of an algal strain used for liquid biofuel production [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Breuer G, Lamers PP, Martens DE, Draaisma RB, Wijffels RH: The impact of nitrogen starvation on the dynamics of triacylglycerol accumulation in nine microalgae strains. Bioresour Technol. 2012, 124: 217-226." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64054e873">17</a>]. Besides nutrient starvation, light stress is an important trigger in the generation of triacylglycerols [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e876">2</a>]. Therefore, both factors were investigated in a combined approach. As in previous work, the model alga <i>C. reinhardtii</i> was used as a control. Cells of both species were inoculated to the same biomass density and cultivated phototrophically for three days to obtain comparable starter cultures (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>, precultures). After three days, cells were harvested and used for comparative analyses. To assess lipid accumulation induced by nutrient starvation, cells were resuspended in nitrogen replete medium or in medium deficient in nitrogen (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>, +N or N, respectively). To assess additional effects related to light availability, two cell densities were chosen for the N samples and designated low density or high density, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>Comparison of growth and lipid accumulation of</b> <b><i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i></b> <b>with</b> <b><i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii.</i></b> <b>(A)</b> Growth under nitrogen replete (+N) and nitrogen deficient (-N) conditions at two different culture densities (high and low density) (n=6). (<b>B)</b> Neutral and polar lipid content as determined after modified Folch extraction and subsequent chromatography after five days of cultivation (n=4). <b>(C)</b> Relative abundances of fatty acids (determined by comparison to internal C17-FA standard set to a value of 100) of <i>M. neglectum</i> (top) and <i>C. reinhardtii</i> (bottom) after nitrogen starvation for five days in low culture densities (-N, low density), (n=4). Error bars indicate standard deviation.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For <i>M. neglectum</i>, low density cultures corresponded to 0.22 g l<sup>-1</sup> (2  10<sup>6</sup>  5  10<sup>5</sup> cells ml<sup>-1</sup>) and high density cultures corresponded to 0.78 g l<sup>-1</sup> (6  10<sup>6</sup>  2  10<sup>6</sup> cells ml<sup>-1</sup>), For the reference strain <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii,</i> similar cell densities were chosen. Biomass accumulation was subsequently monitored and lipid contents as well as fatty acid compositions were determined after five days of cultivation (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>).</p><p>The neutral lipid content (per dry weight) increased from initial 1.40.6% (+N, preculture) to up to 20.94.0% upon nitrogen starvation in low-density cultures in <i>M. neglectum</i> (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>B).</p><p>The overall neutral lipid productivity was determined during the two-step cultivation process for the high density cultures. In this condition, <i>M. neglectum</i> reached maximal productivity rates of 526mg neutral lipids l<sup>-1</sup> day<sup>-1</sup>, which was four times higher compared to <i>C. reinhardtii</i> (134mg neutral lipids l<sup>-1</sup> day<sup>-1</sup>, see Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM1">1</a>: Figure S1), a finding that could, in particular, be attributed to a pronounced increase of biomass in the beginning of the starvation period in the case of <i>M. neglectum</i>. The relative abundance of fatty acids was determined (with an internal C17-FA standard set to a value of 100) to directly compare the individual fatty acid accumulation between <i>M. neglectum</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii. M. neglectum</i> reached comparatively high overall abundances of fatty acids in the total lipid fraction under nitrogen starvation that were roughly 50% higher than those detected in <i>C. reinhardtii</i> (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2). While the total abundance of fatty acid increased only 1.1-fold in high density and 1.5-fold in low density cultures in <i>C. reinhardtii</i> when compared to the nutrient replete control (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2), <i>M. neglectum</i> showed a 2-fold and 2.5-fold increase of its fatty acid content in high and low density cultures respectively (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2). When only the fatty acids of the neutral lipid fraction were considered, this finding became even more evident.</p><p>In <i>M. neglectum</i>, the fatty acid abundances encountered in the neutral lipid fraction of N-starved cells of low density cultures were about tenfold higher in comparison to those of the non-starved cultures (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2). This accumulation pattern is similar to the observation in <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, where fatty acids of the neutral lipid fraction increased more than tenfold (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2) in cultures with lower biomass densities when compared to the nutrient replete control. <i>M. neglectum</i>, therefore, accumulated neutral lipids and fatty acids to high abundances under nitrogen limitation, a feature which was enhanced with increased light penetration. These cultivation parameters also resulted in a positive effect on the accumulation of fatty acids within the neutral lipid fraction in the investigated strains. Interestingly, both strains exhibited a decrease of fatty acids in the polar lipid fraction when nitrogen starvation at low culture densities was applied (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM2">2</a>: Figure S2), an observation also reported for other strains [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1063">2</a>].</p><p>In <i>M. neglectum</i>, the increase of the overall fatty acid content under nitrogen starvation could be mostly attributed to an accumulation of C18:1 and C16:0 which contributed considerably to the neutral lipid fraction (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C). This finding is also reflected by the gravimetrical determinations which show a pronounced increase of the neutral lipid fraction, while the total lipid content increased only moderately (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>B).</p><p>In contrast, <i>C. reinhardtii</i> demonstrated a more diverse accumulation pattern of fatty acids in the neutral lipid fraction under nitrogen starvation, where not only saturated C16 and monoenoic C18 but also C16:4, C18:3 and dienoic C18 abundances increased (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>C).</p><p>It is well established that microalgal biomass and lipid production are strongly dependent on the cultivation conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Chen C-Y, Yeh K-L, Aisyah R, Lee D-J, Chang J-S: Cultivation, photobioreactor design and harvesting of microalgae for biodiesel production: A critical review. Bioresource Technol. 2011, 102 (1): 71-81. 10.1016/j.biortech.2010.06.159." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1090">27</a>]. It was, therefore, important to compare the production rates of <i>M. neglectum</i> with other known oleaginous species in the chosen setup. <i>Parachlorella kessleri</i> and <i>Scenedesmus obliquus</i> were selected for comparative analyses because they represent prominent microalgal species with superior lipid productivities [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Breuer G, Lamers PP, Martens DE, Draaisma RB, Wijffels RH: The impact of nitrogen starvation on the dynamics of triacylglycerol accumulation in nine microalgae strains. Bioresour Technol. 2012, 124: 217-226." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1102">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li X, Pibyl P, Biov K, Kawano S, Cepk V, Zachleder V, kov M, Brnyikov I, Vtov M: The microalga Parachlorella kessleriA novel highly efficient lipid producer. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2012, 110 (1): 97-107." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1106">28</a>]. Low density conditions were applied for all strains to achieve maximum lipid accumulation levels (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 
                          <b>Biomass productivities and lipid content of</b>
                          <b><i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i></b>
                          <b>compared to other microalgal species</b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>When directly compared, <i>M. neglectum</i> demonstrated the highest overall biomass productivity, exceeding those of all three control strains by 33 - 68% under optimal nutrient replete conditions (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>After nitrogen starvation, <i>M. neglectum</i> had a maximal total lipid content of up to 303.4% of dried biomass, significantly higher than in the control strain <i>C. reinhardtii</i> with only 200.8% (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab1">1</a>). The total lipid amount in the oleaginous species <i>P. kessleri</i> and <i>S. obliquus</i> was determined to be higher in this setup; however, the neutral lipid content of <i>M. neglectum</i> was comparable to the top performer <i>S. obliquus.</i> The results demonstrate that neutral lipid production of <i>M. neglectum</i> can be at least equal to <i>P. kessleri</i> and <i>S. obliquus</i>. Therefore, <i>M. neglectum</i> represents a new oleaginous microalgal species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4"><i>M. neglectum</i>shows a robust growth phenotype with a high salt and pH tolerance during phototrophic growth</h3><p>The ability to support growth in brackish or marine environments is an important trait for strains considered for large scale outdoor cultivations. Salt tolerance and cellular adaptation reactions were systematically evaluated in terms of growth, lipid content and composition as well as cell morphology. As shown in Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig2">2</a>A, salt concentrations of 0.5% (w/v) did not affect the biomass accumulation negatively. When salt concentrations were increased to 1%, cells still survived but biomass accumulation was severely reduced.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>Salt tolerance evaluation of</b> <b><i>M. neglectum.</i></b> Cultivations were performed with 0.1% (salt concentration of the standard medium), 0.5% or 1% (w/v) sodium chloride. <b>(A)</b> Biomass accumulation at 350 - 400mol photons m<sup>-2</sup>s<sup>-1</sup> constant light, error bars indicate standard deviation (n=3). <b>(B)</b> Neutral and polar lipid content in percent of dry biomass of two replicates. <b>(C)</b> Light microscopic images, black bar corresponds to 20m.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Neutral lipid contents were found to increase with the induction of salt stress, reaching a maximal content of 8.3% (dry weight) for the cultures grown at 1% salt concentration (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig2">2</a>B). Furthermore, a clear effect of the salt concentration on the cell shape and size was found (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig2">2</a>C). While cells exhibited a mace-like shape with an average size of about 10m at lower salt concentrations, larger round cell clusters of about 25m were formed and sustained when the salt content of the media increased, potentially mitigating the salt stress by decreasing the cellular surface to volume ratio.</p><p>It is well known that the pH can be affected during prolonged microalgal cell cultivation, for example, by secreted fermentation products. For a production strain, tolerance to pH differences can, therefore, be advantageous. In addition, cultivation at higher pH increases the efficiency of CO<sub>2</sub> fixation and may decrease bacterial contamination under non-sterile cultivation conditions. Plate assays were performed to evaluate the sensitivity of <i>M. neglectum</i> to a varying pH range. <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibited tolerance to a wide range of pH conditions and was able to grow between pH5 and pH10 (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig3">3</a>A).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>Investigation of growth on</b> <b><i>M. neglectum</i></b> <b>at different pH and with different antibiotics when grown on agar plates under constant illumination. (A)</b> Test of pH tolerance for <i>M. neglectum</i>. Rows indicate the three biological replicates. <b>(B)</b> Culture sensitivity to antibiotics in ProF media supplemented with 1% glucose. Three different concentrations of (a) kanamycin, (b) hygromycin and (c) paromomycin, control without antibiotics.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The fact that <i>M. neglectum</i> was able to tolerate salt concentrations found in brackish water and was furthermore able to cope with a range of pH conditions demonstrated its generally robust growth characteristics.</p><p>As antibiotics are frequently used in the generation of axenic cultures, as well as selective agents for gene transformation, we investigated whether <i>M. neglectum</i> could tolerate antibiotics commonly used for these strategies (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig3">3</a>B). Concentrations of kanamycin, hygromycin B, and paromomycin ranging from 10-30gml<sup>-1</sup> were applied to plate-level cultures under mixotrophic growth. Although all three substances belong to the group of aminoglycoside antibiotics, significant differences regarding cell toxicity were observed. <i>M. neglectum</i> demonstrated antibiotic resistance against kanamycin with concentrations up to 30g ml<sup>-1</sup>, whereas severe sensitivities to hygromycin B were observed already at 10gml<sup>-1</sup>. The strain also demonstrated resistance to paromomycin. Consequently, powerful antibiotics are available as tools to decontaminate <i>M. neglectum</i> cultures (kanamycin, paromomycin) or as selection reagents for future transformation approaches (hygromycin B).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Full genome sequencing and assembly reveals the diploid character of the <i>M. neglectum</i>genome</h3><p>The genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> was sequenced by next-generation sequencing techniques to elucidate its metabolic pathways and to lay the foundation for a detailed genetic analysis. The genome sequence was obtained using the Illumina MiSeq<sup></sup> technology yielding paired-end reads of 2250 bases in length. Over 8 million paired reads were assembled, resulting in about 6,700 scaffolds: thereof 857 were longer than 20kb (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 
                          <b>Assembly statistics for nuclear draft genome of</b>
                          <b><i>M. neglectum</i></b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The investigation of contig-length vs. read-count (Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM3">3</a>: Figure S3) as performed according to [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wibberg D, Jelonek L, Rupp O, Hennig M, Eikmeyer F, Goesmann A, Hartmann A, Borriss R, Grosch R, Phler A, et al: Establishment and interpretation of the genome sequence of the phytopathogenic fungus Rhizoctonia solani AG1-IB isolate 7/3/14. J Biotechnol. 2013, 167 (2): 142-155. 10.1016/j.jbiotec.2012.12.010." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1742">29</a>] revealed a diploid character of the sequenced genome showing homozygous and heterozygous contigs. To identify the contigs and scaffolds constituting the chloroplast and mitochondrion of <i>M. neglectum</i>, a tBLASTx search was carried out using genes of other known chloroplast and mitochondrial genomes. The chloroplast genome was assembled into a single circular contig with a size of 135,362bp (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig4">4</a>) and a G+C content of 32.38%. The chloroplast genome contains the large single copy (LSC), the small single copy (SSC), as well as inverted repeat (IR) regions (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig4">4</a>). The assembly of the mitochondrial genome led to two contigs separated by gaps (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig5">5</a>). Since the gaps are in-between scaffolded contigs, it is certain that the mitochondrial genome is circular. The mitochondrial genome has a size of approximately 94kb and a G+C content of 45.32%. The remaining scaffolds add to an estimated nuclear genome size of approximately 68Mb with a G+C content of 64.7% (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>Chloroplast genome of</b> <b><i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i></b> <b>.</b> Visualization of the DNA sequence was performed via OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lohse M, Drechsel O, Bock R: OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW): a tool for the easy generation of high-quality custom graphical maps of plastid and mitochondrial genomes. Curr Genetics. 2007, 52 (56): 267-274." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1779">30</a>].</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>Mitochondrial genome of</b> <b><i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i></b> <b>.</b> Visualization of the mitochondrial genome via OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lohse M, Drechsel O, Bock R: OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW): a tool for the easy generation of high-quality custom graphical maps of plastid and mitochondrial genomes. Curr Genetics. 2007, 52 (56): 267-274." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1808">30</a>].</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genome annotation identifies <i>M. neglectum</i>as member of the Selenastraceae with a large mitochondrial genome</h3><p>Gene prediction was performed with different bioinformatics approaches. Prediction by Augustus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Stanke M, Waack S: Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics. 2003, 19 (suppl 2): ii215-ii225." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1832">31</a>], trained with the <i>C. reinhardtii</i> genome, resulted in 13,930 potential genes and prediction by GeneMark-ES [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Borodovsky M, Lomsadze A: Eukaryotic gene prediction using GeneMark.hmm-E and GeneMark-ES. Current Protocols in Bioinformatics. 2002, John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1838">32</a>] in 20,149. Both predictions were analysed by EVidenceModeler [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR: Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 2008, 9 (1): R7-10.1186/gb-2008-9-1-r7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d64054e1841">33</a>], integrating BLASTp protein alignment data with <i>C. reinhardtii</i>. This validation step resulted in a set of 16,845 genes (assuming one transcript per gene) (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab3">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 
                          <b>Gene prediction statistics</b>
                        </b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>During validation, 0.2% of genes predicted by Augustus and 15.4% of genes predicted by GeneMark were rejected by EVidenceModeler. Visualisation of gene predictions and further manual curation of data were performed using the in-house genome annotation system GenDB [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Meyer F, Goesmann A, McHardy AC, Bartels D, Bekel T, Clausen J, Kalinowski J, Linke B, Rupp O, Giegerich R: GenDBan open source genome annotation system for prokaryote genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003, 31 (8): 2187-2195. 10.1093/nar/gkg312." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2117">34</a>], modified for eukaryotic genomes (GenDBE).</p><p>To investigate the chloroplast-specific features of <i>M. neglectum</i>, the chloroplast genome was compared with those of <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and <i>N. gaditana</i> (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig4">4</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM4">4</a>: Figure S4). The chloroplast genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> is similar to <i>N. gaditana</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii</i> concerning the gene content. The three chloroplasts have a common set of 77 orthologous genes, including regions encoding ribosomal RNAs and tRNAs (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig4">4</a>, Additional file <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#MOESM4">4</a>: Figure S4). As in other chloroplast genomes, these genes encode crucial functions of photosynthesis and chloroplast-specific gene expression. Furthermore, all subunits for the synthesis of an active light-independent protochlorophyllide reductase are present.</p><p>The unique sets observed for each chloroplast genome include 12<i>M. neglectum</i>-specific genes, 66<i>N. gaditana</i>-specific genes, and 18 genes specific to the <i>C. reinhardtii</i> chloroplast. Several duplications of genes where found in the <i>M. neglectum</i> chloroplast, such as for the B subunit of ATP synthetase, the photosystem Q(B) protein (psbA), the photosystem I assembly protein (ycf4) and for several tRNAs. In addition, the chloroplast of <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibits specific single copy genes, such as the translation initiation factor IF-1 (encoded by infA).</p><p>Interestingly, the chloroplast genomes of the three algae have a similar gene composition, but only little synteny could be found.</p><p>This finding is supported by the large variance in the chloroplast genome size encountered among sequenced algal genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Simpson CL, Stern DB: The treasure trove of algal chloroplast genomes. Surprises in architecture and gene content, and their functional implications. Plant Physiol. 2002, 129 (3): 957-966. 10.1104/pp.010908." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2178">35</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Odintsova MS, Yurina NP: Genomics and evolution of cellular organelles. Russ J Genet. 2005, 41 (9): 957-967. 10.1007/s11177-005-0187-5." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2181">36</a>]. In contrast, when compared to the chloroplast genomes of algae, conservation of structure and gene content of chloroplasts appears to be greater in plants.</p><p>The mitochondrial genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> is almost six times as large as the mitochondrial genome of <i>C. reinhardtii</i> (16kb); however, it contains fewer genes and extended, potentially non-coding regions. This observation is in accordance with the understanding that algal mitochondrial genomes generally show a high degree of diversity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Nash EA, Nisbet RER, Barbrook AC, Howe CJ: Dinoflagellates: a mitochondrial genome all at sea. Trends Genet. 2008, 24 (7): 328-335. 10.1016/j.tig.2008.04.001." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2193">37</a>]. In total, 17 genes plus 23 tRNAs were identified in the mitochondrial genome of <i>M. neglectum</i>. The basic set of mitochondrial genes is present, including subunits of the NADH dehydrogenase (nad1, nad3, nad4, nad5 and nad6), the cytochrome bc1 complex, cytochrome oxidases (cox2 and cox3) and one subunit of ATP synthase (atp9).</p><p>The 23 tRNAs observed represent an almost complete set of tRNA for translation. Only a threonine-tRNA gene is missing in the mitochondrial genome, together with ribosomal RNA genes. It is well known that the expression of mitochondrial genes is mainly dependent on the nucleus-encoded transcriptional and translational machinery [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Waller RF, Jackson CJ: Dinoflagellate mitochondrial genomes: stretching the rules of molecular biology. Bioessays. 2009, 31 (2): 237-245. 10.1002/bies.200800164." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2202">38</a>].</p><p>The nuclear genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> contains approximately 23 copies of a contig harbouring stable RNA genes including 18S, 28S, and 8S rRNA, as well as 40 tRNAs, and one pseudo-serine tRNA. The 40 tRNAs form a complete set for protein synthesis.</p><p>The overall protein coding sequence lengths were compared between the three algal genomes. <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibits a coding sequence length of 3.156Mb, approximately 16% greater than <i>C. reinhardtii</i> (2.7Mb) and even more than 114% when compared to <i>N. gaditana</i> (1.473Mb). This number is likely to be reduced by future transcriptional investigations.</p><p>To overcome fragmentations, the sequenced <i>Monoraphidium</i> genome was compared with the datasets derived from RNA sequencing approaches of the already well-investigated <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and <i>N. gaditana</i>. The comparative approach, conducted using EDGAR software [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Blom J, Albaum SP, Doppmeier D, Puhler A, Vorholter FJ, Zakrzewski M, Goesmann A: EDGAR: A software framework for the comparative analysis of prokaryotic genomes. Bmc Bioinformatics. 2009, 10: 154-10.1186/1471-2105-10-154." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2236">39</a>], taking into account only genes shared between <i>M. neglectum</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, yielded 4,249 protein coding regions with an average length of 891bp. In addition, <i>M. neglectum</i> and <i>N. gaditana</i> share 2,190 protein coding genes with an average length of 748bp. In comparison, 5,945 proteins could be identified as singletons with an average length of only 191bp. This finding indicates that a comparative approach can be used as a powerful tool to reduce substantially potential false positives gene predictions. These assumptions, however, require further confirmation by future RNA sequencing of <i>M. neglectum</i>.</p><p>Phylogenetic relationship analyses confirmed that <i>M. neglectum</i> clusters within the family of Selenastraceae (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig6">6</a>), but shows a pronounced difference in its 18S rDNA sequence when compared to <i>M. braunii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Krienitz L, Ustinova I, Friedl T, Huss VAR: Traditional generic concepts versus 18S rRNA gene phylogeny in the green algal family Selenastraceae (Chlorophyceae, Chlorophyta). J Phycol. 2001, 37 (5): 852-865. 10.1046/j.1529-8817.2001.01004.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2267">25</a>]. Selenastraceae also cluster closely to the branch of <i>Scenedesmus obliquus</i>, which was used as an oleaginous control species in this work and had been recommended for liquid biofuel applications before [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Breuer G, Lamers PP, Martens DE, Draaisma RB, Wijffels RH: The impact of nitrogen starvation on the dynamics of triacylglycerol accumulation in nine microalgae strains. Bioresour Technol. 2012, 124: 217-226." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2274">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Mandal S, Mallick N: Microalga Scenedesmus obliquus as a potential source for biodiesel production. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2009, 84 (2): 281-291. 10.1007/s00253-009-1935-6." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2277">40</a>].</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>
                          <b>Molecular phylogenetic analysis based on the 18S rDNA sequences using the maximum likelihood method.</b>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Comparative analyses of the predicted gene functions revealed higher gene numbers assigned to carbohydrate metabolism and fatty acid biosynthesis</h3><p>For a deeper investigation of gene functions of <i>M. neglectum</i> in comparison to <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and <i>N. gaditana,</i> we calculated the gene ontology terms for the complete gene-sets of the three genomes using InterPro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hunter S, Jones P, Mitchell A, Apweiler R, Attwood TK, Bateman A, Bernard T, Binns D, Bork P, Burge S, et al: InterPro in 2011: new developments in the family and domain prediction database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012, 40 (D1): D306-D312. 10.1093/nar/gkr948." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2318">41</a>]. In general, most functional categories reveal slightly lower numbers of genes in <i>N. gaditana</i>, probably reflecting the lower total gene number in this organism (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig7">7</a>). Whereas <i>C. reinhardtii</i> shows the highest number of genes in categories such as protein binding, DNA binding, and protein kinase activity, suggesting a larger regulatory repertoire, <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibits a higher gene number in the category catalytic activity.
