This higher abundance of genes encoding putative metabolic functions is reflected in more detail in the categories carbohydrate metabolic process and fatty acid biosynthetic process, in which, <i>M. neglectum</i> again displays the highest gene numbers of the three genomes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Figure 7"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>Gene ontology (GO) assignments for</b> <b><i>M. neglectum</i></b> <b>,</b> <b><i>C. reinhardtii,</i></b> <b>and</b> <b><i>N. gaditana</i></b> <b>.</b> The 35 most extensive GO-terms of the three GO- super categories molecular function, cellular component and biological process are shown. Identification of gene-specific GO-terms was calculated using InterPro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hunter S, Jones P, Mitchell A, Apweiler R, Attwood TK, Bateman A, Bernard T, Binns D, Bork P, Burge S, et al: InterPro in 2011: new developments in the family and domain prediction database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012, 40 (D1): D306-D312. 10.1093/nar/gkr948." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2368">41</a>].</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The attribution of predicted genes to gene families here provides a first, general, overview on gene families in <i>M. neglectum</i>. This, however, needs to be confirmed by more detailed functional and biochemical investigations. An important point to consider is the elimination of pseudogenes that could be identified by techniques such as transcriptome sequencing.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Detailed genome analysis based on lipid pathway reconstruction provides essential new insights into the lipid metabolism of green microalgae</h3><p>Metabolic pathway reconstruction was performed based on the genome data to investigate potential strategies to further improve TAG production. The pathways for fatty acid biosynthesis and the glycerolipid metabolism were reconstructed by comparison of annotated genes with KEGG database assignments. As a result, the key enzymes of the chloroplast fatty acid synthesis pathway have been identified (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig8">8</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Figure 8"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 8</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig8_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig8_HTML.jpg" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p><b>Fatty acid synthesis pathway reconstruction based on sequence similarities.</b> Enzymes are indicated by yellow boxes. Each green circle indicates a homologous gene found for the respective enzyme, metabolites are indicated by light green boxes. ACC: acetyl-CoA carboxylase (EC: 6.4.1.2), MAT: acyl-carrier-protein S-malonyltransferase (EC: 2.3.1.39), KAS3: beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein synthase III (EC: 2.3.1.180), KAR: 3-oxoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein reductase (EC: 1.1.1.100), HD: hydrolyases (EC: 4.2.1.-), EAR: enoyl-acyl-carrier-protein reductase (EC: 1.3.1.-), KAS1/2: beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein synthase I/II (EC: 2.3.1.179, 2.3.1.41), OAH: oleoyl-acyl-carrier-protein hydrolase (EC: 3.1.2.14), PAH: palmitoyl-protein thioesterase (EC: 3.1.2.22). All identified enzymes showed an E-value &lt;10^-10 as determined by PRIAM search described in the methods and materials section.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>These enzymes include the malonyl-CoA:ACP transacylase (MAT, 2.3.1.39), the beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein synthase 3 (KAS3, EC number: 2.3.1.180), the beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein synthase 1/2 (KAS1/2, EC number: 2.3.1.179), the 3-oxoacyl-ACP reductase (KAR, EC number: 1.1.1.100), the beta-hydroxyacyl-ACP dehydrase (HAD, EC number: 4.2.1.), and an enoyl-ACP reductase (EAR, EC 1.3.1.9).</p><p>This set of enzymes is able to catalyse the complete reaction chain from acetyl-CoA via acyl carrier proteins (ACP) to the respective fatty acyl-ACP. Notably, a comparatively high number of homologous genes were identified for acetyl-CoA carboxylase with seven genes (ACC, EC number: 6.4.1.2) and KAR with 11 genes. In addition, homologues have been identified for the fatty acyl-ACP hydrolase (OAH, EC number: 3.1.2.14, PAH, EC number: 3.1.2.22) which cleaves the fatty acid from the acyl carrier protein, releasing the free fatty acid usually as palmitate or stearate, which can then be subjected to several modifications, such as elongation or desaturation. In total, 17 fatty acid desaturase (EC 1.14.19.-) homologues were detected, including five delta-9 desaturase (EC 1.14.19.1) as well as six delta-12 desaturase (EC: 1.14.19.6) homologues.</p><p>In addition to the fatty acid metabolism, genes coding for enzymes of the glycerolipid metabolism [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Wada H, Murata N, Moellering ER, Miller R, Benning C: Molecular genetics of lipid metabolism in the model green alga Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. Lipids in Photosynthesis. Edited by: Govindjee. 2009, Netherlands: Springer, 30: 139-155. 10.1007/978-90-481-2863-1_7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2423">42</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Rismani-Yazdi H, Haznedaroglu BZ, Hsin C, Peccia J: Transcriptomic analysis of the oleaginous microalga Neochloris oleoabundans reveals metabolic insights into triacylglyceride accumulation. Biotechnol Biofuels. 2012, 5: 74-10.1186/1754-6834-5-74." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2426">43</a>] were investigated in more detail. Two genes encoding proteins that show high similarity to acyl-CoA:diacylglycerol acyltransferases type 2 (DGAT2) were identified as well as one encoding a DGAT type 1 homologue. DGAT catalyses the final reaction in TAG generation in the acyl-CoA dependent pathway (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig9">9</a>) and its orthologs have been intensely studied in several phototrophic organisms such as <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Brassica napus,</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="La Russa M, Bogen C, Uhmeyer A, Doebbe A, Filippone E, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Functional analysis of three type-2 DGAT homologue genes for triacylglycerol production in the green microalga Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. J Biotechnol. 2012, 162 (1): 13-20. 10.1016/j.jbiotec.2012.04.006." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2442">44</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Hildebrand M, Abbriano RM, Polle JEW, Traller JC, Trentacoste EM, Smith SR, Davis AK: Metabolic and cellular organization in evolutionarily diverse microalgae as related to biofuels production. Curr Opin Chem Biol. 2013, 17: 506-514. 10.1016/j.cbpa.2013.02.027." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2445">47</a>]. Other identified genes of this pathway include glycerol kinase (GK, EC 2.7.1.30), glycerol-3-phosphate O-acyltransferase (GPAT, EC 2.3.1.15), 1-acylglycerol-3-phosphate O-acyltransferase (AGPAT, EC number: 2.3.1.51), and phosphatidate phosphatase (PP, EC number: 3.1.3.4), which are responsible for the formation of lysophosphatidic and phosphatidic acid, as well as diacylglycerol, respectively (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig9">9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Figure 9"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 9</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig9_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-9-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1186%2F1471-2164-14-926/MediaObjects/12864_2013_Article_5630_Fig9_HTML.jpg" alt="figure9" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p><b>Glycerolipid pathway reconstruction with a focus on TAG formation via the Kennedy pathway reactions.</b> Enzymes are indicated by yellow boxes, each green circle indicates a homologous gene found for the respective enzyme, metabolites are indicated by light green boxes. GK: glycerol kinase (EC: 2.7.1.30), GPAT: glycerol-3-phosphate O-acyltransferase (EC: 2.3.1.15), AGPAT: 1-acylglycerol-3-phosphate O-acyltransferase (EC: 2.3.1.51), PP: phosphatidate phosphatase (EC: 3.1.3.4), DGAT: acyl-CoA:diacylglycerol acyltransferase (EC: 2.3.1.20), PDAT: phospholipid:diacylglycerol acyltransferase (EC: 2.3.1.158), TAG Lipases (EC: 3.1.1.3). All identified enzymes showed an E-value &lt;10^-10 as determined by PRIAM search described in the methods and materials section.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The role of a further acyl-transferase, the phospholipid:diacylglycerol acyltransferase (PDAT), appears highly interesting for TAG metabolism, as reported recently [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Yoon K, Han D, Li Y, Sommerfeld M, Hu Q: Phospholipid:diacylglycerol acyltransferase is a multifunctional enzyme involved in membrane lipid turnover and degradation while synthesizing triacylglycerol in the unicellular green microalga Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. The Plant Cell Online. 2012, 24 (9): 3708-3724. 10.1105/tpc.112.100701." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2475">48</a>]. PDAT mediates an acyl-CoA independent pathway to generate TAG. It was shown that PDAT of <i>C. reinhardtii</i> is able to utilise multiple substrates and performs various enzymatic reactions, ranging from TAG synthesis via transacylation of DAG with phospholipid or galactolipid acyl groups to transacylation of two DAG [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Yoon K, Han D, Li Y, Sommerfeld M, Hu Q: Phospholipid:diacylglycerol acyltransferase is a multifunctional enzyme involved in membrane lipid turnover and degradation while synthesizing triacylglycerol in the unicellular green microalga Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. The Plant Cell Online. 2012, 24 (9): 3708-3724. 10.1105/tpc.112.100701." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2481">48</a>].</p><p>PDAT was shown not only to be able to hydrolyse phospholipids, galactolipids and cholesteryl esters but also TAG, therefore, attributing an important function in membrane turnover as well as TAG synthesis and degradation to this enzyme. When the genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> was analysed for PDAT homologues, two candidates were found, with one (Mneg_03131.t1) showing a conserved phosphatidylcholine-sterol O-acyltransferase domain (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig9">9</a>), representing interesting potential targets for future biotechnological engineering approaches.</p><p>Since <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibits a strong response to nitrogen depletion, the regulation of genes involved in TAG generation as well as in the breakdown of polar lipids is of special interest. A nitrogen response regulator (NRR) has been identified in the genome of <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and was shown to be involved in neutral lipid accumulation under nitrogen depletion, as well as in the regulation of DGAT type 1 and type 2 expression [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Boyle NR, Page MD, Liu B, Blaby IK, Casero D, Kropat J, Cokus S, Hong-Hermesdorf A, Shaw J, Karpowicz SJ, et al: Three acyltransferases and a nitrogen responsive regulator are implicated in nitrogen starvation-induced triacylglycerol accumulation in Chlamydomonas. J Biol Chem. 2012, 287 (19): 15811-15825. 10.1074/jbc.M111.334052." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2502">45</a>]. A potential homologue of this regulator was found in the genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> (Mneg_07163.t1) which also exhibits a putative SQUAMOSA promoter-binding-like domain, and could, therefore, play a role in the nitrogen stress response of <i>M. neglectum</i>.</p><p>The successful reconstruction of the metabolic pathways of fatty acid and glycerolipid synthesis clearly demonstrate the integrity and quality of the genome sequencing and annotation data. These results provide new insights into the lipid metabolism of green microalgae, presenting the opportunity to identify targets for future metabolic engineering that could increase the neutral lipid production potentials of <i>M. neglectum</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">The characterization of <i>M. neglectum</i>as a robust phototrophic oleaginous strain highlights its potential suitability for oil-based biofuel production</h3><p>Stress induction by nitrogen starvation and enhanced light penetration are well-known mechanisms and driving forces for increasing neutral lipid accumulation in many microalgal strains [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Hu Q, Sommerfeld M, Jarvis E, Ghirardi M, Posewitz M, Seibert M, Darzins A: Microalgal triacylglycerols as feedstocks for biofuel production: perspectives and advances. Plant J. 2008, 54 (4): 621-639. 10.1111/j.1365-313X.2008.03492.x." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2534">2</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Solovchenko A: Physiological role of neutral lipid accumulation in eukaryotic microalgae under stresses. Russian J Plant Physiol. 2012, 59 (2): 167-176. 10.1134/S1021443712020161." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2537">49</a>]. <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibits a distinct accumulation of neutral lipids and the fatty acids of this fraction as response to nitrogen starvation (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab1">1</a>, Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>B, and C). The observation that low culture densities even increase this effect (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig1">1</a>B), underlines the importance of light intensity for reaching optimal lipid accumulations in particular for this strain.</p><p>The importance of light for optimal lipid accumulation was already demonstrated for other algae such as <i>Nannochloropsis sp</i>. [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rodolfi L, Zittelli GC, Bassi N, Padovani G, Biondi N, Bonini G, Tredici MR: Microalgae for oil: strain selection, induction of lipid synthesis and outdoor mass cultivation in a low-cost photobioreactor. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2009, 102 (1): 100-112. 10.1002/bit.22033." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2559">15</a>] and <i>Parachlorella kessleri</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li X, Pibyl P, Biov K, Kawano S, Cepk V, Zachleder V, kov M, Brnyikov I, Vtov M: The microalga Parachlorella kessleriA novel highly efficient lipid producer. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2012, 110 (1): 97-107." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2565">28</a>]. However, other investigations with <i>Chlorella minutissima</i> and <i>Dunaliella tertiolecta</i> revealed no significant increase of lipids in various culture dilutions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Tang H, Chen M, Simon Ng KY, Salley SO: Continuous microalgae cultivation in a photobioreactor. Biotechnol Bioengineer. 2012, 109 (10): 2468-2474. 10.1002/bit.24516." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2575">50</a>], suggesting species-specific differences in their glycerolipid metabolism.</p><p>Of particular note is that <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibited a comparatively high accumulation of biomass even within the nitrogen starvation period, performing clearly better than the model organism <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and the previously investigated <i>M. contortum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2590">19</a>]. The neutral lipid productivity of <i>M. neglectum</i> determined in this work (52mgl<sup>-1</sup> day<sup>-1</sup>) is relatively high when compared to multiple other strains under photoautotrophic growth conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Chen C-Y, Yeh K-L, Aisyah R, Lee D-J, Chang J-S: Cultivation, photobioreactor design and harvesting of microalgae for biodiesel production: A critical review. Bioresource Technol. 2011, 102 (1): 71-81. 10.1016/j.biortech.2010.06.159." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2601">27</a>], but appears low in comparison to other data considering <i>Nannochloropsis sp.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rodolfi L, Zittelli GC, Bassi N, Padovani G, Biondi N, Bonini G, Tredici MR: Microalgae for oil: strain selection, induction of lipid synthesis and outdoor mass cultivation in a low-cost photobioreactor. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2009, 102 (1): 100-112. 10.1002/bit.22033." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2607">15</a>] and <i>Nannochloropsis gaditana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2614">21</a>]. Lipid productivities of up to 204mgl<sup>-1</sup> day<sup>-1</sup> were reported under nitrogen deprivation for <i>Nannochloropsis sp.</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Rodolfi L, Zittelli GC, Bassi N, Padovani G, Biondi N, Bonini G, Tredici MR: Microalgae for oil: strain selection, induction of lipid synthesis and outdoor mass cultivation in a low-cost photobioreactor. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2009, 102 (1): 100-112. 10.1002/bit.22033." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2624">15</a>], while <i>N. gaditana</i> showed productivities of up to 310mgl<sup>-1</sup> day<sup>-1</sup>[<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2635">21</a>]. It is however important to note that a comparison of these numbers can be misleading, since the cultivation conditions applied in the <i>Nannochloropsis</i> work mentioned before were considerably different to the conditions applied here, with respect to varying initial biomass densities, differences in illumination and potential stress caused by outdoor cultivation conditions. This issue is further emphasized by the fact that another <i>Monoraphidium</i> strain, which was suggested as potential feedstock for biodiesel production before, showed lipid productivities of 149mgl<sup>-1</sup> d<sup>-1</sup> in heterotrophic and only 7mgl<sup>-1</sup> d<sup>-1</sup> in autotrophic conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Yu X, Zhao P, He C, Li J, Tang X, Zhou J, Huang Z: Isolation of a novel strain of Monoraphidium sp. and characterization of its potential application as biodiesel feedstock. Bioresource Technol. 2012, 121: 256-262." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2653">24</a>]. For accurate evaluation of <i>M. neglectum</i>, three control strains were used in this work, <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, <i>P. kessleri</i>, and <i>S. obliquus</i>. Higher TAG levels were reported for <i>S. obliquus</i> and <i>P. kessleri</i> strains in recent publications in comparison to our findings [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Breuer G, Lamers PP, Martens DE, Draaisma RB, Wijffels RH: The impact of nitrogen starvation on the dynamics of triacylglycerol accumulation in nine microalgae strains. Bioresour Technol. 2012, 124: 217-226." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2675">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Pibyl P, Cepk V, Zachleder V: Production of lipids in 10 strains of Chlorella and Parachlorella, and enhanced lipid productivity in Chlorella vulgaris. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2012, 94 (2): 549-561. 10.1007/s00253-012-3915-5." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2678">51</a>], indicating a clear potential to further optimize the lipid productivity of <i>M. neglectum</i> by systematic improvements of the culturing conditions, which was not in the focus of this work. As lipid productivities are highly dependent on biomass concentrations, higher productivities for <i>M. neglectum</i> are likely to be attainable through further optimization studies emphasizing optimised biomass densities and light penetration [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Breuer G, Lamers PP, Martens DE, Draaisma RB, Wijffels RH: The impact of nitrogen starvation on the dynamics of triacylglycerol accumulation in nine microalgae strains. Bioresour Technol. 2012, 124: 217-226." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2688">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Li X, Pibyl P, Biov K, Kawano S, Cepk V, Zachleder V, kov M, Brnyikov I, Vtov M: The microalga Parachlorella kessleriA novel highly efficient lipid producer. Biotechnol Bioeng. 2012, 110 (1): 97-107." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2691">28</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Pibyl P, Cepk V, Zachleder V: Production of lipids in 10 strains of Chlorella and Parachlorella, and enhanced lipid productivity in Chlorella vulgaris. Appl Microbiol Biotechnol. 2012, 94 (2): 549-561. 10.1007/s00253-012-3915-5." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2694">51</a>].</p><p>The lipid contents determined for the oleaginous control strains <i>P. kessleri</i> and <i>S. obliquus</i> in this work were in the same range as for <i>M. neglectum</i>, demonstrating that the potential of all three species for liquid biofuels production can be regarded as being similar. C18:1 and C16:0 were the major fatty acids that accumulated in <i>M. neglectum</i> after nutrient starvation, corresponding well to previous results from the related species <i>M. contortum</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2716">19</a>], suggesting similar mechanisms of lipid accumulation in both organisms. In particular, oleic acid is known to accumulate under nitrogen starvation in many species such as <i>Ankistrodesmus braunii</i>, <i>C. vulgaris, Chlorella zofingiensis</i>, and <i>S. obliquus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Breuer G, Lamers PP, Martens DE, Draaisma RB, Wijffels RH: The impact of nitrogen starvation on the dynamics of triacylglycerol accumulation in nine microalgae strains. Bioresour Technol. 2012, 124: 217-226." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2728">17</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Williams VR, McMillan R: Lipids of Ankistrodesmus braunii. Science. 1961, 133 (3451): 459-460. 10.1126/science.133.3451.459." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2731">52</a>]. This is in contrast to <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, where more unsaturated fatty acids are common, contributing significantly to the more diverse fatty acid pattern under nitrogen starvation. This could indicate that, in <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, membrane recycling and conversion of polar lipids to triacylglycerols plays a more prominent role [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Boyle NR, Page MD, Liu B, Blaby IK, Casero D, Kropat J, Cokus S, Hong-Hermesdorf A, Shaw J, Karpowicz SJ, et al: Three acyltransferases and a nitrogen responsive regulator are implicated in nitrogen starvation-induced triacylglycerol accumulation in Chlamydomonas. J Biol Chem. 2012, 287 (19): 15811-15825. 10.1074/jbc.M111.334052." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2741">45</a>] when compared to <i>M. neglectum</i>.</p><p>The high levels of C18:1 and saturated C16 in <i>M. neglectum</i> under nitrogen starvation are a promising fatty acid profile for biofuel production which encourages the cultivation of this strain in large-scale concepts for the production of biofuels, preferably in outdoor bioreactors. This composition, however, would most likely require further blending with oil from other sources to meet the requirements for liquid biofuels such as biodiesel [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schenk PMP, Thomas-Hall SR, Evan S, Marx U, Mussgnug JH, Posten C, Kruse O, Hankamer B: Second generation biofuels: high-efficiency microalgae for biodiesel production. BioEnergy Res. 2008, 1 (1): 20-43. 10.1007/s12155-008-9008-8." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2754">1</a>].</p><p>The productivity in outdoor cultivation conditions very much relies on the robustness of the strain used. Parameters defining robustness include tolerance to changing light intensity, temperature, pH, or salinity. Several studies have been performed on salt and pH tolerance of various microalgal strains [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Takagi M, Karseno Y, Yoshida T: Effect of salt concentration on intracellular accumulation of lipids and triacylglyceride in marine microalgae Dunaliella cells. J Biosci Bioengineer. 2006, 101 (3): 223-226. 10.1263/jbb.101.223." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2760">53</a><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Santos AM, Janssen M, Lamers PP, Evers WAC, Wijffels RH: Growth of oil accumulating microalga Neochloris oleoabundans under alkalinesaline conditions. Bioresource Technol. 2012, 104: 593-599." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2763">55</a>]. Some adapted marine organisms such as <i>Dunaliella tertiolecta</i> tolerate salt concentrations up to three times higher than sea water and show an increased lipid content under these conditions [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Takagi M, Karseno Y, Yoshida T: Effect of salt concentration on intracellular accumulation of lipids and triacylglyceride in marine microalgae Dunaliella cells. J Biosci Bioengineer. 2006, 101 (3): 223-226. 10.1263/jbb.101.223." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2769">53</a>]. These values were outstanding and could not be reached with other organisms, though some strains such as <i>Navicula sp.</i> are known to require at least salt concentrations similar to sea water to retain their highest biomass productivity [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Matsumoto M, Sugiyama H, Maeda Y, Sato R, Tanaka T, Matsunaga T: Marine Diatom, Navicula sp. strain JPCC DA0580 and marine green alga, Chlorella sp. strain NKG400014 as potential sources for biodiesel production. Appl Biochem Biotechnol. 2010, 161 (1): 483-490." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2776">56</a>]. In this context, <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibited robust phototrophic growth in salt concentrations up to 0.5% sodium chloride in the medium (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig2">2</a>) which is comparatively high when compared to other freshwater strains [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Salama E-S, Kim H-C, Abou-Shanab RI, Ji M-K, Oh Y-K, Kim S-H, Jeon B-H: Biomass, lipid content, and fatty acid composition of freshwater Chlamydomonas mexicana and Scenedesmus obliquus grown under salt stress. Bioproc Biosyst Engineer. 2013, 1-7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2785">54</a>]. This tolerance to salinity points towards efficient adaptation mechanisms to different salt concentrations in the investigated range. Together with the observed tolerance against high pH ranges (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig3">3</a>), it is feasible to suggest that <i>M. neglectum</i> has robust growth characteristics likely suitable for outdoor cultivation.</p><p>A further distinct feature of <i>M. neglectum</i> is the increase in cell size when cultivated under brackish water conditions (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig2">2</a>C) which could potentially reduce energy costs of harvesting during downstream processing [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Milledge J, Heaven S: A review of the harvesting of micro-algae for biofuel production. Rev Environ Sci Biotechnol. 2013, 12 (2): 165-178. 10.1007/s11157-012-9301-z." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2803">57</a>].</p><p>Although currently no transformation strategy exists for this strain, the sequencing of its genome will allow the elucidation of regulatory regions which may become important in the construction of transformation vectors. The identification of the commonly used antibiotic hygromycin B as a selective agent within this work will also aid in future genetic manipulation attempts with this organism.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">The genome of <i>M. neglectum</i>reveals a highly diverse repertoire of genes encoding catalytic activities in carbohydrate and fatty acid metabolism</h3><p>For accessing the full potential of this alga as a potential biotechnological production host, the genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> was sequenced, assembled and annotated and eventually compared to existing data derived from other green microalgae and oleaginous Heterokontophyta. It could be concluded that <i>M. neglectum</i> represents a highly interesting genome which can be used for comparative analyses to further elucidate parameters for efficient neutral lipid synthesis in microalgae. With an estimated genome size of 68 Mbp (Table<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Tab2">2</a>), the <i>M. neglectum</i> genome is considerably larger than the genome of the Heterokontophyte <i>Nannochloropsis gaditana</i> (ca. 30Mb, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2836">21</a>]), but smaller than the 121Mb genome of the green alga <i>C. reinhardtii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2842">8</a>]. However, <i>C. reinhardtii</i> (version 1.1) and <i>M. neglectum</i> have similar gene numbers, indicating that only a small fraction of the genome can be assigned to coding sequences. This percentage is 16.7% in <i>C. reinhardtii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2855">8</a>] and slightly higher in the <i>M. neglectum</i> genome (25.1%)<i>.</i></p><p>The genomic G+C content of 64.74% of <i>M. neglectum</i> is similar to that of <i>C. reinhardtii</i> and <i>Chlorella variabilis</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2875">8</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Blanc G, Duncan G, Agarkova I, Borodovsky M, Gurnon J, Kuo A, Lindquist E, Lucas S, Pangilinan J, Polle J, et al: The Chlorella variabilis NC64A genome reveals adaptation to photosymbiosis, coevolution with viruses, and cryptic sex. Plant Cell. 2010, 22 (9): 2943-2955. 10.1105/tpc.110.076406." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2878">58</a>], but considerably higher than that of <i>N. gaditana</i> (54% [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2885">21</a>]). Lower G+C-contents have also been reported for <i>Cyanidioschyzon</i> and <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i> (see [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2894">8</a>], and references therein).</p><p>The size of the chloroplast was determined to be 135,362bp, therefore, within the typical range of chloroplast genome sizes of algae and plants [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Chan CX, Gross J, Yoon HS, Bhattacharya D: Plastid origin and evolution: new models provide insights into old problems. Plant Physiol. 2011, 155 (4): 1552-1560. 10.1104/pp.111.173500." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2900">59</a>]. The gene content of the chloroplast genome is similar to that of other known algal chloroplasts. Surprisingly, the mitochondrial genome differs significantly in length from other known algal genomes. With an estimated length about 96 kbp, at present the <i>M. neglectum</i> mitochondrial genome represents the largest known mitochondrial genome in algae. The largest stramenopile mitochondrial genome had been identified in the diatom <i>Phaeodactylum tricornutum</i> (77.4 kbp, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Oudot-Le Secq M-P, Green BR: Complex repeat structures and novel features in the mitochondrial genomes of the diatoms Phaeodactylum tricornutum and Thalassiosira pseudonana. Gene. 2011, 476 (12): 20-26." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2909">60</a>]). Certain plant species were shown to harbour longer mitochondrial genomes, which can reach sizes of &gt;2 Mbp, such as found in <i>Cucumis melo</i> and <i>Cucumis sativus</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Nash EA, Nisbet RER, Barbrook AC, Howe CJ: Dinoflagellates: a mitochondrial genome all at sea. Trends Genet. 2008, 24 (7): 328-335. 10.1016/j.tig.2008.04.001." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2919">37</a>], considerably larger than <i>M. neglectum</i>. Moreover, the mitochondrial genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> also exhibits a rather small gene density, therefore, resulting in a similar or even lower gene number than observed in other algae, including <i>C. reinhardtii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2931">8</a>] or <i>N. gaditana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2938">21</a>].</p><p>Research using haploid-dominant algae has for decades benefitted from ease of transformation and rapid forward and reverse genetics with <i>C. reinhardtii</i> or <i>Volvox carteri</i> as prominent examples [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Coll J: Review. Methodologies for transferring DNA into eukaryotic microalgae. Span J Agric Res. 2006, 4 (4): 316-330. 10.5424/sjar/2006044-209." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2950">61</a>]. In contrast, the sequenced genome of <i>M. neglectum</i> was found to be diploid, similar to several other green algae like <i>Dunaliella salina</i> or <i>Haematococcus pluvialis</i> and several diatoms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Coll J: Review. Methodologies for transferring DNA into eukaryotic microalgae. Span J Agric Res. 2006, 4 (4): 316-330. 10.5424/sjar/2006044-209." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2963">61</a>]. This finding indicates that, compared to <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, future genetic transformation strategies will need to address the emergence of hemizygous individuals. Alternatively, further elucidation of the life cycle of <i>M. neglectum</i> is recommended to allow potentially easier transformation if generation of haploid cells is possible.</p><p>The total number of predicted genes in the <i>M. neglectum</i> genome is 16,845, which is considerably higher than in <i>N. gaditana</i> (9,052 genes, [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2982">21</a>]). A similar number of genes were predicted for <i>C. reinhardtii</i> (15,143 gene predictions before RNA sequencing [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Merchant SS, Prochnik SE, Vallon O, Harris EH, Karpowicz SJ, Witman GB, Terry A, Salamov A, Fritz-Laylin LK, Marechal-Drouard L, et al: The Chlamydomonas genome reveals the evolution of key animal and plant functions. Science. 2007, 318 (5848): 245-250. 10.1126/science.1143609." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d64054e2988">8</a>]). It has to be noted that the determination of gene numbers is dependent on the prediction algorithm, its training sets, and the inclusion of other data sources such as transcriptome data. Transcriptome studies have not been performed on <i>M. neglectum</i> yet and are desirable to validate the predicted genes.</p><p>The differential analysis of the gene content of <i>M. neglectum, C. reinhardtii</i>, and <i>N. gaditana</i> via gene ontology (GO) allowed further insights into the degree of conservation and diversity of algal genomes. In all three investigated genomes, an over-representation of specific functional categories was found, including ATP binding, protein binding, and catalytic activity (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig7">7</a>). These over-representations may indicate the trend to expand genomes towards regulatory mechanisms, which could be in particular the case for <i>C. reinhardtii</i>. Interestingly, <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibited a higher number of genes in categories related to fatty acid biosynthesis, lipid metabolic processes and carbohydrate metabolism, suggesting a higher versatility or functional redundancy when compared to the other two organisms. The reconstruction of the metabolic pathways, therefore, provided more detailed information.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">The reconstruction of lipid pathways in <i>M. neglectum</i>reveals new insights into fatty acid and neutral lipid synthesis</h3><p>The reconstruction of the metabolic network concerning fatty acid and glycerolipid metabolism was a crucial precondition to interpret the biochemical data. This reconstruction was also valuable to gain important insights into the lipid metabolism of <i>M. neglectum</i> to identify potential genetic engineering targets in neutral lipid synthesis. The high number of homologous genes identified for acetyl CoA carboxylase (ACC), 3-oxoacyl-ACP reductase (KAR), and beta-ketoacyl-acyl-carrier-protein synthase (KAS1/2), suggests a more pronounced role of these factors for the lipid metabolism in <i>M. neglectum</i> compared to the green alga <i>C. reinhardtii</i>. This functional redundancy could promote the comparatively fast accumulation of fatty acids in <i>M. neglectum</i>, leading potentially to an intense use of the chloroplast pathway for producing TAG precursors.</p><p>Though the characterization of the <i>M. neglectum</i> genome also reveals the presence of a broad variety of desaturases, the fatty acid analysis of nitrogen starved cultures resulted in the identification of mainly palmitic acid (C16:0) and oleic acid (C18:1), contrasting to a high diversity of fatty acids in <i>C. reinhardtii</i>. The very distinct fatty acid production in <i>M. neglectum</i>, however, represents a very promising profile for biodiesel production from fatty acid methyl esters (FAMEs), where a high degree of saturation is desired [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Schenk PMP, Thomas-Hall SR, Evan S, Marx U, Mussgnug JH, Posten C, Kruse O, Hankamer B: Second generation biofuels: high-efficiency microalgae for biodiesel production. BioEnergy Res. 2008, 1 (1): 20-43. 10.1007/s12155-008-9008-8." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3049">1</a>]. Further analysis of the lipid metabolism identified the existence of a distinct gene coding for a palmitoyl-protein thioesterase and three further homologues for oleoyl-ACP hydrolases (Figure<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#Fig8">8</a>). Since thioesterases were reported to potentially reduce feedback inhibition in fatty acid biosynthesis in diatoms [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Gong Y, Guo X, Wan X, Liang Z, Jiang M: Characterization of a novel thioesterase (PtTE) from Phaeodactylum tricornutum. J Basic Microbiol. 2011, 51 (6): 666-672. 10.1002/jobm.201000520." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3056">62</a>], the abundance of these enzymes could be considered as a potential bottleneck for efficient fatty acid production and consequently could be putative targets for improving the fatty acid production in microalgae by genetic engineering when genetic tools for <i>M. neglectum</i> become available.</p><p>To elucidate the role of the thioesterases in bypassing feedback inhibition, it will be of special interest to investigate their gene expression during nitrogen starvation. It has to be noted that the central carbon metabolism of diatoms shows some pronounced differences compared to green algae [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Hockin NL, Mock T, Mulholland F, Kopriva S, Malin G: The response of Diatom central carbon metabolism to nitrogen starvation is different from that of green algae and higher plants. Plant Physiol. 2012, 158 (1): 299-312. 10.1104/pp.111.184333." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3065">63</a>], therefore, the data from this work open up the opportunity for further comparative studies between Chlorophyta and diatoms to investigate the metabolic fluxes and their regulation towards fatty acid production, culminating in neutral lipid synthesis.</p><p>In addition to fatty acid synthesis, the abundance of triacylglcerols (TAGs) is a deciding factor for the suitability of a microalga for oil-based biofuel production.</p><p>The analysis of the <i>M. neglectum</i> genome and the reconstruction of the glycerolipid metabolism revealed the presence of only three DGAT type 1 and 2 homologs, which appears unusually low when compared to <i>C. reinhardtii</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="La Russa M, Bogen C, Uhmeyer A, Doebbe A, Filippone E, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Functional analysis of three type-2 DGAT homologue genes for triacylglycerol production in the green microalga Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. J Biotechnol. 2012, 162 (1): 13-20. 10.1016/j.jbiotec.2012.04.006." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3080">44</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Boyle NR, Page MD, Liu B, Blaby IK, Casero D, Kropat J, Cokus S, Hong-Hermesdorf A, Shaw J, Karpowicz SJ, et al: Three acyltransferases and a nitrogen responsive regulator are implicated in nitrogen starvation-induced triacylglycerol accumulation in Chlamydomonas. J Biol Chem. 2012, 287 (19): 15811-15825. 10.1074/jbc.M111.334052." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3083">45</a>] or the oleaginous Heterokontophyte <i>N. gaditana</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Radakovits R, Jinkerson RE, Fuerstenberg SI, Tae H, Settlage RE, Boore JL, Posewitz MC: Draft genome sequence and genetic transformation of the oleaginous alga Nannochloropis gaditana. Nat Commun. 2012, 3: 686-" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3090">21</a>]. The comparably low number of DGAT homologs indicates that this pathway for TAG generation could <i>in vivo</i> be less adaptive in <i>M. neglectum</i>. Therefore, the DGAT genes represent highly interesting targets for comparative functional studies. Together with the distinct accumulation of C16:0 and C18:1 fatty acids in <i>M. neglectum</i> under nitrogen starvation, this finding furthermore points towards the important role of the chloroplast pathway for the synthesis of neutral lipid precursors [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Fan J, Andre C, Xu C: A chloroplast pathway for the de novo biosynthesis of triacylglycerol in Chlamydomonas reinhardtii. FEBS lett. 2011, 585 (12): 1985-1991. 10.1016/j.febslet.2011.05.018." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3102">64</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13"><i>M. neglectum</i>can serve as a model organism to elucidate the distinct role of PDAT enzymes in TAG accumulation and homeostasis</h3><p>When the acyl-Co independent mechanism of TAG generation is addressed, the presence of two PDAT homologues was confirmed with one of the candidates (Mneg_03131.t1) carrying a distinct functional acyltransferase domain. Interestingly, this is, to our knowledge, the first time that such a PDAT has been identified in the genome of an oleaginous green microalga. While PDAT homologues could be found in the transcriptome of the marine strain <i>Dunaliella tertiolecta</i>, they appear to be absent in <i>Neochloris oleoabundans</i> [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Rismani-Yazdi H, Haznedaroglu BZ, Hsin C, Peccia J: Transcriptomic analysis of the oleaginous microalga Neochloris oleoabundans reveals metabolic insights into triacylglyceride accumulation. Biotechnol Biofuels. 2012, 5: 74-10.1186/1754-6834-5-74." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3122">43</a>].</p><p>It is of particular note that the lipid accumulation pattern of <i>M. neglectum</i> exhibits several similarities to the oleaginous green microalga <i>N. oleoabundans</i> such as the increase of FAMEs and specifically of C18:1 (up to about 55%), while retaining a similar percentage of C16:0 (approx. 23%) of the fatty acid composition under nitrogen starvation [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Rismani-Yazdi H, Haznedaroglu BZ, Hsin C, Peccia J: Transcriptomic analysis of the oleaginous microalga Neochloris oleoabundans reveals metabolic insights into triacylglyceride accumulation. Biotechnol Biofuels. 2012, 5: 74-10.1186/1754-6834-5-74." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3134">43</a>]. Since PDAT is potentially not present or expressed in <i>N. oleoabundans</i>, a comparison of these two closely related species represents a highly interesting target for further investigations to elucidate its metabolic function at normal growth conditions and under different stresses. These findings also shed light on the diversity of lipid metabolism in the branch of green algae, as it has been noted before [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Liu B, Benning C: Lipid metabolism in microalgae distinguishes itself. Curr Opin Biotechnol. 2013, 24 (2): 300-309. 10.1016/j.copbio.2012.08.008." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3140">11</a>].</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec14" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec14-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec14">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec14-content"><p>This investigation provides a foundational analysis of <i>M. neglectum</i>, a member of the Selenastraceae, which exhibits robust growth characteristics as well as relatively high lipid yields. Biomass and lipid production analyses presented in this work clearly demonstrate that <i>M. neglectum</i> has a high potential to serve as a production strain for future liquid biofuel concepts. This strain demonstrates favourable characteristics for cultivation including robust photoautotrophic growth, high neutral lipid contents containing an interesting profile of fatty acids during nitrogen starvation, pH tolerance, as well as a large cell phenotype at higher salt concentrations. A further increase of lipid yields can be expected when growth conditions are systematically optimised.</p><p>Genomic annotation and feature analysis elucidated key features of the lipid synthesis pathway, providing new insights into the evolution and physiological differences regarding the conversion of carbon dioxide into energy-dense glycerolipids in microalgae. The nuclear genome exhibits comparatively high redundancy in fatty acid synthesis processes, while the number of homologues involved in the acyl-CoA dependent TAG generation is considerably smaller, therefore, presenting potential targets for future genetic engineering approaches. The genomic analyses presented in this work will aid in the identification of potential bottlenecks for neutral lipid synthesis and provide the basis for future genetic engineering strategies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Cultivation conditions</h3><p><i>Monoraphidium neglectum</i> (SAG 48.87) and <i>Scenedesmus obliquus</i> (SAG 276-6) were obtained from the Algae Collection in Gttingen, Germany, while <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> (CC1690) was obtained from the <i>Chlamydomonas</i> Resource Center, University of Minnesota, US, and <i>Parachlorella kessleri</i> (211/11H) from the CCAP, United Kingdom. Cultivations were performed in Provasoli based minimal media (ProF) as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3186">19</a>]. Cells were cultivated in 20ml minimal media in Erlenmeyer flasks on a rotary shaker and used for inoculation of 3l roller flasks adjusted to an OD<sub>750</sub> of 0.05. Growth was monitored by daily OD<sub>750</sub> measurements as well as biomass development and manually counted cell number. Cultivations were performed under constant illumination with white light at intensities ranging between 350-400mol photons m<sup>-2</sup>s<sup>-1</sup>. Media were aerated with air enriched with 3% CO<sub>2</sub>. After 3 days, cells were harvested and transferred to 400ml batch cultures.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Nitrogen starvation and stress tolerance</h3><p>For the induction of lipid accumulation, cultures were transferred to media with the same composition but lacking a nitrogen source as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3208">19</a>]. To assess general cultivation conditions of the second stage a further batch of 1:4 diluted cultures was transferred to nutrient replete conditions as control. Biomass was harvested after five days of cultivation, lyophilized and stored at -80C prior to further investigations.</p><p>Salt tolerance was investigated in liquid cultures containing 0.1, 0.5, 1.0 and 2.0% (w/v) sodium chloride. Performance of cultures was monitored by measurements of OD<sub>750</sub> and the dry biomass weight. Before cultures reached the stationary phase, the biomass was harvested, lyophilized and subjected to lipid extraction and chromatographic analysis.</p><p>The pH sensitivity of <i>M. neglectum</i> was tested in plate assays. 5ml of cell suspension (adjusted to OD<sub>750</sub> =0.2) were spotted on agar plates containing Provasoli freshwater medium with additional 0.59M thiamine, 4.1 nM biotin and 0.6 nM cobalamin. For pH3.0, the Provasoli medium (adjusted to pH3.0) and agar were separately autoclaved and combined after cooling down to about 60C, while for pH5.0 - 7.5 Provasoli media were adjusted to the respective pH before agar was added and autoclaved. For plates with pH10, each stock solution for Provasoli (with CaCl<sub>2</sub> diluted to 1/10 of the original concentration) as well as double distilled water containing the agar and sodium chloride were autoclaved separately and combined at a temperature of about 60 - 70C. For mixotrophic cultivation on agar, 10gL<sup>-1</sup> glucose was added to media after autoclaving by filter sterilisation. Vitamins were added after media were cooled down to about 60C.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Lipid extractions and chromatographies</h3><p>Extractions were performed in two technical replicates per biological replicate from 50mg lyophilized biomass. After homogenization (3 x 30s at 6,500rpm using a Precellys 24, Peqlab, Erlangen, Germany), the total lipids were extracted according to a modified Folch protocol [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Folch J, Lees M, Stanley GHS: A simple method for the isolation and purification of total lipides from animal tissues. J Biol Chem. 1957, 226 (1): 497-509." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3236">65</a>] using a total of 4ml methanol and 8ml chloroform. Contaminants were removed by washing the extract with 3ml of deionised water. From the dried total lipid extract, column chromatographies were performed to separate the neutral from the polar lipid fraction as described elsewhere [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Li Y, Han D, Hu G, Dauvillee D, Sommerfeld M, Ball S, Hu Q: Chlamydomonas starchless mutant defective in ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase hyper-accumulates triacylglycerol. Metab Engineer. 2010, 12 (4): 387-391. 10.1016/j.ymben.2010.02.002." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3239">66</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">FAME analysis</h3><p>Neutral and polar lipids were transesterified to fatty acid methyl esters (FAME) as described previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Bogen C, Klassen V, Wichmann J, Russa ML, Doebbe A, Grundmann M, Uronen P, Kruse O, Mussgnug JH: Identification of Monoraphidium contortum as a promising species for liquid biofuel production. Bioresour Technol. 2013, 133: 622-626." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3250">19</a>]. Spectra analysis was performed with Xcalibur. The peak identity was confirmed by comparison of retention times and mass spectra to a 37 FAME mix (47885-U, Supelco). Unidentified peaks were analysed on their mass spectra and attributed to fatty acids according to their fragmentation pattern. To determine the relative amount of fatty acids within the total lipid fraction, 50g C17-triacylglycerol (glyceryl triheptadecanoate, Sigma-Aldrich, Steinheim, Germany) were added to each sample as an internal standard. Total ion chromatograms were recorded and used to calculate the relative abundances of the individual fatty acid after normalization to the internal standard (set to 100, dry biomass basis).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">DNA isolation and sequencing</h3><p>DNA was extracted using the cetyltrimethylammonium bromide method as reported previously [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Murray MG, Thompson WF: Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Res. 1980, 8 (19): 4321-4326. 10.1093/nar/8.19.4321." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3262">67</a>]. After an RNAse digest (Ribonuclease A, Roth, Germany), the quality was controlled in a 1% agarose gel.</p><p>The sequencing was performed on an Illumina MiSeq machine with sequencing libraries prepared using the Illumina Nextera DNA Sample kit. DNA fragments of a size between 500 and 700 base pairs were cut from an agarose gel and purified with a MinElute Gel Extraction Kit (Qiagen). DNA amount and quality were monitored on an Agilent Bioanalyzer. The sequencing was performed using the MiSeq Reagent Kit v2 (Illumina) with 2250 cycles.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Genome assembly and gene annotation</h3><p>All reads obtained by genome sequencing were assembled to contigs and scaffolds using the Newbler assembler version 2.6 (Roche) with settings for heterozygous genomes. Nuclear and organelle genomes were assembled manually using the compatible finishing package Consed version 23.0 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Gordon D: Viewing and Editing Assembled Sequences Using Consed. Current Protocols in Bioinformatics. 2003, John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc, 2:11.2.1-11.2.43." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3275">68</a>].</p><p>The annotation of the three genomes (chloroplast, mitochondrion and nuclear) was done by a specific annotation pipeline, which consists of three steps. All potential genes were predicted by two <i>ab initio</i> gene prediction tools: Augustus [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Stanke M, Waack S: Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics. 2003, 19 (suppl 2): ii215-ii225." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3284">31</a>] with the <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i> genome as training set and in parallel with GeneMark-ES (version 2) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Borodovsky M, Lomsadze A: Eukaryotic gene prediction using GeneMark.hmm-E and GeneMark-ES. Current Protocols in Bioinformatics. 2002, John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3290">32</a>], which combines GeneMark.hmm for prediction of eukaryotic genomes with a self-training procedure. In addition, a protein alignment with all <i>C. reinhardtii</i> proteins was performed. To evaluate over 34,000 predicted genes the software EVidenceModeler (EVM) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Haas BJ, Salzberg SL, Zhu W, Pertea M, Allen JE, Orvis J, White O, Buell CR, Wortman JR: Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 2008, 9 (1): R7-10.1186/gb-2008-9-1-r7." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3297">33</a>] was used to filter the gene set and to eliminate putative false positive predictions. For that purpose we assigned different weightings for the different prediction outputs. Augustus and GeneMark were assigned with the same higher weight (2) and the protein alignment a lower weight (1). Ribosomal RNAs were identified by using the RNAmmer 1.2 server [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Lagesen K, Hallin P, Rdland EA, Strfeldt H-H, Rognes T, Ussery DW: RNAmmer: consistent and rapid annotation of ribosomal RNA genes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2007, 35 (9): 3100-3108. 10.1093/nar/gkm160." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3300">69</a>] and tRNAs were determined by tRNAscan-SE version 1.21 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Schattner P, Brooks AN, Lowe TM: The tRNAscan-SE, snoscan and snoGPS web servers for the detection of tRNAs and snoRNAs. Nucleic Acids Res. 2005, 33 (suppl 2): W686-W689." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3303">70</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Lowe TM, Eddy SR: tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 1997, 25 (5): 0955-0964." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3306">71</a>]. Organellar genomes were analysed and printed with OGDRAW [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lohse M, Drechsel O, Bock R: OrganellarGenomeDRAW (OGDRAW): a tool for the easy generation of high-quality custom graphical maps of plastid and mitochondrial genomes. Curr Genetics. 2007, 52 (56): 267-274." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3309">30</a>]. All gene information were integrated to the annotation platform GenDBE, which is a modification of GenDB for the annotation of eukaryotic genomes [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Meyer F, Goesmann A, McHardy AC, Bartels D, Bekel T, Clausen J, Kalinowski J, Linke B, Rupp O, Giegerich R: GenDBan open source genome annotation system for prokaryote genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003, 31 (8): 2187-2195. 10.1093/nar/gkg312." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3312">34</a>]. GenDBE allows the manual curation of gene specific annotation in addition to the visualisation of gene order.</p><p>This Whole Genome Shotgun project has been deposited at DDBJ/EMBL/GenBank under the accession AYTC00000000. The version described in this paper is version AYTC01000000.</p><p>For genome comparison based on GO categories gene specific GO-terms were obtained by analysing all genes of the three genomes <i>C. reinhardtii</i>, <i>N. gaditana</i> and <i>M. neglectum</i> in Interpro [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Hunter S, Jones P, Mitchell A, Apweiler R, Attwood TK, Bateman A, Bernard T, Binns D, Bork P, Burge S, et al: InterPro in 2011: new developments in the family and domain prediction database. Nucleic Acids Res. 2012, 40 (D1): D306-D312. 10.1093/nar/gkr948." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3329">41</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>The evolutionary history was inferred by using the Maximum Likelihood method based on the Tamura-Nei model [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Tamura K, Nei M: Estimation of the number of nucleotide substitutions in the control region of mitochondrial DNA in humans and chimpanzees. Mole Biol Evol. 1993, 10 (3): 512-526." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3340">72</a>], with the tree of the highest log likelihood (-6385.4692) shown. Initial trees were obtained by the Neighbor-Joining method to a matrix of pairwise distances, estimated using the Maximum Composite Likelihood (MCL) approach. The tree is drawn to scale. The branch lengths are measured in number of substitutions per site. Numbers indicate bootstrap values after 5,000 replications. The analysis involved 12 nucleotide sequences with a total of 1284 positions in the final dataset. Evolutionary analyses were conducted in MEGA5 [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Tamura K, Peterson D, Peterson N, Stecher G, Nei M, Kumar S: MEGA5: Molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mole Biol Evol. 2011, 28 (10): 2731-2739. 10.1093/molbev/msr121." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3343">73</a>].</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Metabolic pathway reconstruction</h3><p>Pathway reconstruction was performed for FA and TAG synthesis by means of PathwayTools and PRIAM. The KEGG pathway proposed on the gene set of <i>M. neglectum</i> by GenDBE was analysed using PRIAM (database profile of March 2013) [<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Meyer F, Goesmann A, McHardy AC, Bartels D, Bekel T, Clausen J, Kalinowski J, Linke B, Rupp O, Giegerich R: GenDBan open source genome annotation system for prokaryote genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003, 31 (8): 2187-2195. 10.1093/nar/gkg312." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3357">34</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="ClaudelRenard C, Chevalet C, Faraut T, Kahn D: Enzymespecific profiles for genome annotation: PRIAM. Nucleic Acids Res. 2003, 31 (22): 6633-6639. 10.1093/nar/gkg847." href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d64054e3360">74</a>]. Therefore, the E.C. numbers of the proposed KEGG pathway were used as basis for the PRIAM search to identify corresponding genes with an E-value cutoff of 1*10<sup>-10</sup>.</p></div></div></section>

                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/10.1186/1471-2164-14-926/email/correspondent/c1/new">Olaf Kruse</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="additional-information" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="additional-information-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="additional-information">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="additional-information-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3><p>The authors declare that they have no competing interests.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Authors contributions</h3><p>The research was designed by CB, AAD, JHM, JK and OK. CB, AAD, JW, MG, KL, OBK performed the research, with the main part of this work carried out by the first author, CB. CB performed growth analysis, lipid analysis and FAME profiling under nutrient replete conditions and nitrogen starvation, was involved in genome sequencing, performed phylogenetic analysis, and <i>in silico</i> reconstruction of lipid metabolism, prepared therefore figures and tables and drafted the manuscript. JW investigated growth under nitrogen starvation and performed lipid analysis. MG performed investigations of salt tolerance, KJL of resistance to antibiotics and OBK of pH tolerance. Genome sequencing was performed by AA, genome assembly and annotation by AA and AAD. The GENDBE project was created and maintained by OR and AG. Manual gene assignments and comparison of genomes was carried out by AAD. AAD prepared figures and tables related to genome assembly and metabolic reconstruction. Data analysis and interpretation: CB, AAD, JHM, OK. Manuscript writing: CB, AAD, JK, JHM, OK. All authors read and approved the final manuscript.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec24" data-title="Electronic supplementary material"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec24-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec24">Electronic supplementary material</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec24-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div>
